In [3]:
# PART 1

# 1. NER-tag your texts in NLTK and SpaCy and provide totals for each NER tag in both systems. 
# (Please also include the total number of tokens for your corpus in NLTK and SpaCy.)
# 2. Provide a few examples for each type of tag (if possible).
# 4. Provide examples of different NER tags from your text for the same word/token as tagged in NLTK and SpaCy.
# 5. In a short paragraph, (~5 sentences), provide your thoughts on the NER tags in both NLTK and SpaCy and the strengths and weaknesses of each NER tagging system. Please also comment on the strengths and weaknesses, and the potential utility of NER as a whole.


from urllib import request
url = "https://www.gutenberg.org/files/1399/1399-0.txt"
response = request.urlopen(url)
raw = response.read().decode('utf8')
AK_text=raw[840:1000840] # Getting rid of the extra info from Guttenberg.
In [4]:
AK_tok = nltk.word_tokenize(AK_text) 
len(AK_tok)
Out[4]:
215186
In [5]:
# So, total number of tokens is 215189. Now let's go into NER
In [6]:
AK_pos = nltk.pos_tag(AK_tok)
AK_chunks = nltk.ne_chunk(AK_pos)
for chunk in AK_chunks:
    if hasattr(chunk, 'label'): #if the item has a label
        print(chunk.label(), ' '.join(c[0] for c in chunk))

In [7]:
# That gives us a first taste. Seems to be doing a pretty good job, except for some confusion about the tag
# (See "Matvey" --a Russian surname-- usually correctly tagged as "PERSON" but in some cases confused as an
# "ORGANIZATION". Some funny errors: "GPE": "God", "PERSON": "Liberalism", and Russian diminutives seem to 
# completely throw the tagger off, ex. g: "Grisha", "Tanya", "Stiva", "Penza" all marked as "GPE")

# frequency of each NE type
nltk_AK_labels = [] #an empty list
for chunk in AK_chunks:
    if hasattr(chunk, 'label'): 
        nltk_AK_labels.append(chunk.label()) #add the label to the list
pprint(Counter(nltk_AK_labels)) #the Counter function tallies the count of each label in the list

Counter({'PERSON': 4152,
         'GPE': 743,
         'ORGANIZATION': 404,
         'FACILITY': 22,
         'GSP': 2})

import en_core_web_sm

nlp = spacy.load("en_core_web_sm")

len(AK_doc)

for ent in AK_doc.ents: #ents calls a list of named entities in tuple format
    print(ent.text, ent.start_char, ent.end_char, ent.label_) #the entity name, start character, 
    #end character and label

In [11]:
# I'll try to be brief, but it might be difficult because lots of interesting things are going on here.
# First of all, let's get the obvious out of the way:

# 1. It's clear that NER --both in NLTK and SpaCy-- are very English-coded. What I mean by that is that, although
# I do not know fully how NE are detected and then tagged, it's clear that Russian names are not being recognized
# at all in most cases. Let me use an example; when Anna Karenina's name and patronimic --Anna Arkadyevna-- appear
# it gets recognized as a name. Notice that the same doesn't happen with her husband's name, Stepan Arkadyevitch,
# who gets confused with an "ORGANIZATION". I bet this is because the tagger recognizes that "Arkadyevna" is
# dependent on "Anna" and "Anna" is unproblematically identified as a personal name (it is known in English),
# whereas "Stepan" is not so clearly a name (from an English persepctive). The moment "Arkadyevitch" or "Arkadyevna"
# feature alone, they get systematically confused with "ORGANIZATION", as happens with most patronimics, some
# some surnames (like "Oblonsky") or diminutives (like "Grisha")
# This is no criticsm from my part to the developers of the taggers, it just goes to show that the linguistic 
# phenomenon is incredibly connected with the culture of its speakers (no Russian would have any trouble at all 
# identifying those names) and that connection is essential to the correct understanding of linguistic elements,
# yet difficult to code.

# 2. The Spacy tagger is a lot more complete (has more categories) and most of them are useful, like its ability to
# distinguish between "German" or "English" as a nationality ("NORP) vs. a language ("LANGUAGE"). In a work like
# Tolstoy's, where characters switch to French or German depending on class and context, this could be very useful
# to quickly detect how Tolstoy portrayed languages being used to mark social class.
# It's also interesting to see how Spacy tags "DATE" vs. "TIME", usually successfully (although some errors still
# take place: "only a year" is tagged as "DATE", when it should be "TIME", since "TIME" reflects duration). Overall
# it's very impressive. "WORK_OF_ART" is a noble effort, but seems to be failing spectacularly (it has tagged 
# "they've", "Mamma", "Darya Alexandrovna", "Yes, Sir", "Everything's in confusion", as works of art, perhaps the 
# linguistic criteria to qualify as such should be more restrictive)

# As answers to the specific questions asked:
# - "God" is mistagged as "GPE" in NLTK but more accuratedly tagged as "PERSON" by Spacy
# - "Levin" is frequently mistagged as "GPE" in NLTK and correctly tagged as "PERSON" by Spacy (Russian names!)
# - NLTK is unable to detect any entity that is not capitalized, whereas Spacy was able to detect "bald watchmaker",
# quite an impressive feat.
# - NLTK thinks "Wiesbaden" is a "PERSON", Spacy knows it's a "GPE" (a German city)

# The numbers of each tag are all over the place. I would not even bother paying too much attention to those provided
# by NLTK, since it's really full of mistagged entities. Spacy's numbers are probably more accurate, not to mention
# that they are also more specific --even if NLTK was working fine, "GPE", "LANGUAGE", and "LOC" could all be 
# grouped under NLTK's "GPE"
In [12]:
# 3. Find the 2 longest sentences in your text (either by number of tokens or raw length) and provide visual "trees" for them in either NLTK and SpaCy.

from nltk.tokenize import sent_tokenize, word_tokenize 

sentences = sent_tokenize(AK_text)
word_count = lambda sentence: len(word_tokenize(sentence))
long_sentence= max(sentences, key=word_count) # the longest sentence by word count
print(long_sentence)

Why it was the three young ladies had one
day to speak French, and the next English; why it was that at certain
hours they played by turns on the piano, the sounds of which were
audible in their brothers room above, where the students used to work;
why they were visited by those professors of French literature, of
music, of drawing, of dancing; why at certain hours all the three young
ladies, with Mademoiselle Linon, drove in the coach to the Tversky
boulevard, dressed in their satin cloaks, Dolly in a long one, Natalia
in a half-long one, and Kitty in one so short that her shapely legs in
tightly-drawn red stockings were visible to all beholders; why it was
they had to walk about the Tversky boulevard escorted by a footman with
a gold cockade in his hatall this and much more that was done in their
mysterious world he did not understand, but he was sure that everything
that was done there was very good, and he was in love precisely with
the mystery of the proceedings.

# Good. I'll be cutting all this to the last sentence, from the hyphen onwards. Mostly because I'm even unsure about
# the right parsing and dependencies of all those indirect questions (I've done syntactic parsing in other languages
# but not English)

tree1 = nltk.Tree('NP', ['All', 'this'])
tree2=nltk.Tree('NP', ['much', 'more'])
tree3=nltk.Tree('NP', ['that'])
tree4=nltk.Tree('VP', ['was','done'])
tree5=nltk.Tree('NP', ['in', 'their', 'mysterious', 'world'])
tree6=nltk.Tree('NP', ['he'])
tree7=nltk.Tree('VP', ['did', 'not', 'understand'])
tree8=nltk.Tree('NP', ['he'])
tree9=nltk.Tree('VP', ['was', 'sure'])
tree10=nltk.Tree('NP', ['that', 'everything'])
tree11=nltk.Tree('NP', ['that'])
tree12=nltk.Tree('VP', ['was', 'done', 'there'])
tree13=nltk.Tree('VP', ['was', 'very', 'good'])
tree14=nltk.Tree('NP', ['he'])
tree15=nltk.Tree('VP', ['was', 'in', 'love', 'precisely'])
tree16=nltk.Tree('NP', ['with', 'the', 'mystery', 'of', 'the', 'proceedings'])
In [17]:
tree17=nltk.Tree('SubS', [tree3, tree4, tree5]) #SubS, it's a subordinate sentence acting as a NP
tree2=nltk.Tree('NP', ['much', 'more', tree17])
tree18=nltk.Tree('NP', [tree1,'and', tree2])
tree19=nltk.Tree('S1', [tree6, tree7, tree18])
tree20=nltk.Tree('SubS', [tree11, tree12]) #SubS, it's a subordinate sentence acting as a NP
tree10=nltk.Tree('NP', ['that', 'everything', tree20])
tree21=nltk.Tree('NP', [tree10, tree13])
tree22=nltk.Tree('S2', [tree8, tree9, tree21])
tree23=nltk.Tree('S3', [tree14, tree15, tree16])
tree24=nltk.Tree('S', [tree19, ',', 'but', tree22, ',', 'and', tree23])
tree24.draw()
print(tree24)

(S
  (S1
    (NP he)
    (VP did not understand)
    (NP
      (NP All this)
      and
      (NP
        much
        more
        (SubS (NP that) (VP was done) (NP in their mysterious world)))))
  ,
  but
  (S2
    (NP he)
    (VP was sure)
    (NP
      (NP that everything (SubS (NP that) (VP was done there)))
      (VP was very good)))
  ,
  and
  (S3
    (NP he)
    (VP was in love precisely)
    (NP with the mystery of the proceedings)))

# I'll be using the opening sentence of Anna Karenina, and then I'll split the sentence I made the tree for in two.
# This way I'll have (according to my feelings):

# Sent1: "Happy families are all alike; every unhappy family is unhappy in its own way" --> negative
# Sent2: "All this and much more that was done in their mysterious world he did not understand" --> neutral
# Sent3: "But he was sure that everything that was done there was very good, and he was in love precisely with
# the mystery of the proceedings."--> positive

# Ok, got it. The closer polarity is to 1, the more positive the sentence (and the other way round).
# The higher the subjectivity, the more the algorithm is sure that the sentence expresses a feeling 
# (vs. test_sent2, which is devoid of feeling because it's just a fact.)

# I don't even know if that went better or worse than I was expecting. Clearly Sent1 is tricky: the polarity does not
# even pass the threshold of 0.1, yet it is detected that it's full of "sentiment/feeling". I guess this counts
# like an enormous failure? As in, the algorithm is saying that it knows the sentence is expressing *a lot* of
# sentiment (with a measure of 0.95/1.0), but it's unable to figure out *which* sentiment?
# Sent3 is the closest to what I would say: a relatively positive sentence, and yes, it is expressing feeling 
# (albeit I'd say it is expressing a lot more feeling than just 0.77)
# Sent2 has a low polarity, which matches my expectations, but perhaps too much feeling --in my opinion.
# I'm mostly fascinated by Sent1 and if that was the result with Tolstoy, I'd really wonder what would happen with
# an author like Gogol, who is attributed with creating "laugher in between tears"; reading any of his works is
# really one of those experiencies where you're really unsure about whether you should laugh at the characters
# or cry with them.



# PART 2

# 5. Write a tag pattern to cover noun phrases that contain gerunds,
# e.g. "the/DT receiving/VBG end/NN", "assistant/NN managing/VBG editor/NN". 
# Add these patterns to the grammar, one per line. Test your work using some tagged sentences of your own devising.
In [35]:
sentence = [("the", "DT"), ("receiving", "VBG"), ("end", "NN"),
... ("and", "CC"), ("assistant", "NN"), ("managing", "VBG"), ("editor", "NN"), ("the", "DT"), ("running", "VBG"), ("man", "NN"), ("and", "CC"),
            ("senior", "NN"), ("working", "VBG"), ("MOD", "NN")]

grammar = r"""
  NP: {<DT*.>?<NN*.>?<VBG*.><NN*.>}                     
  CLAUSE: {<NP>}           
  """

cp = nltk.RegexpParser(grammar)
result = cp.parse(sentence)
print(result)

(S
  (CLAUSE (NP the/DT receiving/VBG end/NN))
  and/CC
  (CLAUSE (NP assistant/NN managing/VBG editor/NN))
  (CLAUSE (NP the/DT running/VBG man/NN))
  and/CC
  (CLAUSE (NP senior/NN working/VBG MOD/NN)))

# 6. Write one or more tag patterns to handle coordinated noun phrases, 
# e.g. "July/NNP and/CC August/NNP", "all/DT your/PRP$ managers/NNS and/CC supervisors/NNS", 
# "company/NN courts/NNS and/CC adjudicators/NNS".
In [37]:
sentence=[("July", "NNP"), ("and", "CC"), ("August", "NNP")]
sentence2= [("all", "DT"), ("your", "PRP$"), ("managers", "NNS"), ("and", "CC"), ("supervisors", "NNS")]
grammar = r"""
  NP: {<DT*.>?<PR*.>?<NN*.><CC*.><NN*.>}                     
  CLAUSE: {<NP>}           
  """

cp = nltk.RegexpParser(grammar)
result = cp.parse(sentence)
print(result)

(S (CLAUSE (NP July/NNP and/CC August/NNP)))
